Ruling on Partner's Unilateral Use of Shared Canal Water
Ruling on sharing canal water between partners and its proof via qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a partner diverting shared irrigation water to land without a share, and explain the qiyas proof using the invalidity of water rights as mahr.

Core Idea
A partner cannot unilaterally use shared water for land outside the partnership; water rights are not freely transferable.

This lesson examines a specific case of partnership in irrigation canals. The text establishes that water rights (shurb) are restricted to the defined share; surplus cannot be repurposed without consent. The author strengthens the ruling by drawing an analogy with the prohibition of using water rights as mahr in marriage, showing that water rights are not a freely transferable commodity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify the act: a partner wants to take water from a shared canal to land that has no share in that canal. State the ruling: the text says 'اجازت نہیں ہوگی' and 'جائز نہیں' meaning it is not permissible (haram).
2
Note the two scenarios
First scenario: taking water directly to land without a share. Second scenario: after the partner's own land is filled, using surplus for other land. Both are ruled impermissible. Understand that the prohibition covers all unauthorized use.
3
Examine the parallel case
The text gives a similar case: a partner living upstream cannot close holes to change the distribution from hole-based (quantity) to day-based (time). This reinforces that unilateral alteration of distribution method is not allowed.
4
Analyze the proof: qiyas with mahr
The proof is qiyas: just as water rights cannot be validly set as mahr in marriage because they are not a known, specific property (مال معلوم), similarly water rights cannot be sold or transferred without the consent of all partners. Understand the wajh al-dalalah: the common illa is that water rights are not a fully determinate property.
5
Consider the implication
The ruling implies that water rights (shurb) in a partnership are attached to the defined land and cannot be repurposed or transferred unilaterally. All partners must consent to any change in use.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرکاء میں سے کوئی شریک نہر کا پانی دوسری زمین میں لے جانا چاہے جس کا شرب اس نہر میں نہیں، اجازت نہیں ہوگی۔ اگر پہلی زمین بھر جائے اسی سے دوسری زمین کو لگانا چاہے تو بھی جائز نہیں۔ مسئلہ مذکورہ کی نظیر: اوپر کی زمین میں رہنے والا شریک بعض سوراخوں کو بند کرنا چاہے تو اسے یہ حق حاصل نہیں، سوراخوں کے ذریعے تقسیم کو ایام کی تقسیم میں تبدیل کرنے کا حق نہیں۔ پہلے مسئلہ کی دلیل: شرب کو بطور مہر مقرر کیا گیا تو یہ تسمیہ درست نہیں، مہر مثل واجب ہے، صاحب شرب کے قرض میں شرب کو بیچا نہیں جائے گا۔
If one partner among the partners wants to take the water of a canal to another land that does not have a share in that canal, permission will not be given. If the first land is filled and he wants to irrigate the second land from it, even then it is not permissible. A similar case: A partner living in the upper land wants to close some holes; he does not have this right. He does not have the right to convert the division through holes into a division by days. Proof of the first issue: If the right to water is fixed as mahr, then this specification is not valid; proper mahr is obligatory. The right to water will not be sold for the debt of the owner of the right to water.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling regarding shared water rights in a canal. The Hanafi position is that if multiple partners share a canal, no single partner may unilaterally divert water to land that has no share in that canal. Even if the partner's own land is fully irrigated, he cannot then use the surplus to water other land he owns but which is not part of the share. The text then provides a parallel case: a partner living upstream cannot close off certain holes (openings) in the canal to change the distribution method from one based on holes (quantity) to one based on days (time). The author then provides the legal analogy (dalil): just as water rights cannot be used as a valid mahr in marriage (because water rights are not a known property suitable for mahr), similarly, water rights cannot be sold to repay a debt. This establishes that water rights are not freely transferable without the consent of all partners.

Key Terms
شرب (shurb): Right to irrigation water from a canal or well.
مہر (mahr): Dower given by husband to wife in marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a partner's own land is fully irrigated, may he use the surplus water to irrigate another land he owns that has no share in the canal?
A) Yes, because it is his water after his share is filled.
B) No, it is not permissible.
C) Yes, if he pays compensation to the other partners.
D) No, but only if the other partners object.

Key Takeaway
A partner cannot unilaterally divert shared canal water to land without a share, even if his own share is satisfied, because water rights are attached to specific land and require collective consent.

Hunting with Trained Animals: Permissibility and Conditions
Stating the permissibility of hunting with trained dogs, cheetahs, and falcons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for hunting with trained animals and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the dog eating from the prey.

Core Idea
Hunting with trained animals is permissible, but the prey becomes forbidden if the dog eats from it.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi rulings on hunting with trained predatory animals (al-jawaarih). The passage from al-Hidayah lays out the permissibility, conditions, and a key disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions regarding the dog eating from the prey. Understanding this dispute reveals how textual proofs (Quran and Hadith) are weighed in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is hunting with trained animals such as dogs, cheetahs, and falcons. The ruling is permissibility (mubah) under specific conditions.
The proof is Quran 5:4.
2
Note the conditions
The animal must be trained (mu'allam) and the hunter must recite tasmiyah (Bismillah) upon releasing it.
Without these, the prey is not halal.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
If the trained dog eats from the prey, does the prey become forbidden? Imam Abu Hanifa said it remains halal; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it becomes haram.
The text declares the Sahibayn's view preponderant (al-rajih).
4
Understand the evidence
The Quran (5:4) permits hunting with trained animals generally. The Hadith of al-Sa'b ibn Jaththamah specifically forbids eating prey if the dog eats from it, as it caught for itself.
The Sahibayn rely on the Hadith; Abu Hanifa used analogy (qiyas) with slaughter.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is that of the Sahibayn: the prey is forbidden if the dog eats from it.
The text says 'al-rajih qawl al-sahibayn'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الجوارح: كَلْبٌ، فَهْدٌ، بَازِي، وَجَمِيعُ الْجَوَارِحِ الْمُعَلَّمَةِ مِنْهَا الصَّيْدُ جَائِزٌ. وَالدَّلِيلُ قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى: { وَمَا عَلَّمْتُمْ مِنَ الْجَوَارِحِ مُكَلِّبِينَ } (المائدة: 4). وَشَرْطُهُ: أَنْ يَكُونَ الْجَارِحُ مُعَلَّمًا، وَأَنْ يُرْسِلَهُ الصَّائِدُ وَيُسَمِّيَ. فَإِنْ أَرْسَلَ كَلْبًا مُعَلَّمًا أَوْ بَازِيًا مُعَلَّمًا وَقَالَ: بِسْمِ اللَّهِ، فَقَتَلَهُ، حَلَّ. وَإِنْ أَكَلَ الْكَلْبُ مِنْ الصَّيْدِ لَمْ يُؤْكَلْ، لِأَنَّهُ يَدُلُّ عَلَى أَنَّهُ لَمْ يُمْسِكْهُ لِصَاحِبِهِ بَلْ لِنَفْسِهِ. فَإِنْ أَخَذَ كَلْبٌ صَيْدًا وَأَكَلَ مِنْهُ بَعْضًا ثُمَّ مَاتَ الصَّيْدُ، لَا يُؤْكَلُ. وَإِنْ شَرِبَ دَمَهُ، أُكِلَ. وَقَالَ الصَّاحِبَانِ: إِذَا أَكَلَ الْكَلْبُ مِنَ الصَّيْدِ حَرُمَ، وَالدَّلِيلُ حَدِيثُ الصَّعْبِ بْنِ جَثَّامَةَ: { أَنَّ النَّبِيَّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ سُئِلَ عَنْ صَيْدِ الْكَلْبِ يَأْكُلُ مِنْهُ، فَقَالَ: لَا تَأْكُلْ فَإِنَّهُ لَمْ يُمْسِكْ عَلَيْكَ إِنَّمَا أَمْسَكَ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ }. وَقَالَ أَبُو حَنِيفَةَ: يُؤْكَلُ إِنْ أَكَلَ الْكَلْبُ مِنْهُ، لَكِنَّ الرَّاجِحَ قَوْلُ الصَّاحِبَيْنِ. وَالْحَاصِلُ: أَنَّ الصَّيْدَ يَحِلُّ إِذَا زَكَّاهُ الْكَلْبُ أَوِ الْبَازِي بِجَرْحِهِ، وَيَحْرُمُ إِذَا أَكَلَ مِنْهُ الْكَلْبُ أَوْ لَمْ يَفْعَلْ شَرْطَ التَّسْمِيَةِ.
Section on [hunting] animals: dog, cheetah, falcon, and all trained predatory animals – hunting with them is permissible. The proof is His statement: { And what you have taught of the hunting animals, training them } (Al-Ma'idah: 4). Its condition: that the hunting animal be trained, and that the hunter releases it and utters the tasmiyah. If he releases a trained dog or trained falcon and says 'Bismillah', and it kills, it is permissible. If the dog eats from the prey, it is not eaten because that indicates it caught it for itself, not for its owner. If a dog catches prey and eats part of it, then the prey dies, it is not eaten. If it drinks its blood, it is eaten. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If the dog eats from the prey, it becomes forbidden – the proof is the hadith of al-Sa'b ibn Jaththamah: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) was asked about the prey of a dog that eats from it; he said: Do not eat, for it did not catch for you, rather it caught for itself.' Abu Hanifa said: It is eaten if the dog eats from it, but the preponderant view is the statement of the two companions. The summary: the prey is permissible when the dog or falcon ritually slaughters it by wounding it, and forbidden if the dog eats from it or if the condition of tasmiyah is not fulfilled.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on hunting (Kitab al-Sayd), specifically the section on the tools and animals used for hunting (al-jawaarih). It begins by stating the ruling that hunting with trained predatory animals such as dogs, cheetahs, and falcons is permissible. The primary proof is the Quranic verse (Al-Ma'idah: 4) which allows hunting with trained animals. The passage then outlines conditions: the animal must be trained (mu'allam), and the hunter must recite the tasmiyah (Bismillah) upon releasing it. If these conditions are met, the prey becomes halal. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scenario where the dog eats from the prey: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that it remains permissible, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule it becomes haram, citing a hadith. The mufta bihi (relied upon) position is that of the Sahibayn, as indicated by the text ('al-rajih'). Additional details: if the dog drinks the blood but does not eat the meat, the prey is permissible; if it eats any part, it becomes haram. The passage also clarifies that the animal must cause a wound (jarh) to the prey, as the hunting animal acts as a tool of dhabh.

Key Terms
الجوارح (al-jawaarih): Predatory animals and birds trained for hunting, such as dogs, cheetahs, and falcons
معلم (mu'allam): Trained; an animal that responds to commands and catches prey for the hunter, not for itself
تسمية (tasmiyah): Uttering 'Bismillah' (in the name of Allah) when releasing the hunting animal

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a trained dog drinks the blood of the prey but does not eat its meat?
A) The prey is haram
B) The prey is halal
C) The prey is halal only if the dog is trained
D) The prey is halal only if the hunter recites tasmiyah

Key Takeaway
Hunting with trained animals is permissible under conditions, but the mufta bihi is that the prey becomes forbidden if the dog eats from it.

Hunting with Trained Animals: The Ruling of Halal Game with Tasmiyah
States ruling that trained dog/bird with tasmiyah makes hunted game halal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game caught by a trained animal, explain the condition of tasmiyah, and apply the analogy of slaughter to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
A trained hunting animal is like a knife — reciting tasmiyah makes the game halal.

The permissibility of hunting with trained animals is established in the Quran and elaborated by Hanafi jurists. This lesson examines the ruling that if a trained dog or bird is released with tasmiyah and wounds the game, the meat is halal. The key analogy is to slaughter: the animal is an instrument, like a knife. We will also note an intra-Hanafi difference regarding witnessing the kill.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is hunting with a trained dog (kalb mu'allim) or trained hawk (baz mu'allim). The ruling is that the game becomes halal (permissible to eat) provided certain conditions are met.
The ruling is a statement of permissibility (ibahah).
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the Quranic verse in Surah al-Ma'idah (5:4) which permits eating what trained animals catch. The Hanafi school also uses qiyas (analogy) to slaughter with a knife — both are instruments that cause death when properly used.
The dalil is both naqli (Quran) and aqli (qiyas).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Quranic phrase 'wa ma allamtum min al-jawarih' is interpreted as allowing the meat if the animal is trained and tasmiyah is recited. The qiyas to a knife emphasizes that the trained animal is merely an instrument; just as a knife does not eat the meat, the trained animal should not eat from the prey to be considered valid.
The analogy hinges on the instrument-like nature of the trained animal.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is an intra-Hanafi disagreement about whether the hunter must witness the kill. Some scholars require the hunter to see the death or wounding; others (the Sahibayn) do not, as long as the animal is trained and tasmiyah was recited.
This difference is an example of legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that witnessing is not required. The game is halal even if the hunter did not see the exact moment of death, provided the conditions of training and tasmiyah are fulfilled. This is the view of the Sahibayn and is adopted by later authoritative texts like Al-Hidayah.
The mufta bihi is the position given in the source passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمر ہ اختلاف اور صاحبین کی دلیل کلب معلم یا بازی معلم تسمیہ پڑھ کر چھوڑ دیا اس نے جا کر شکار کو زخمی کیا یا مر گیا تو یہ شکار حلال ہے چھری کی طرح کتا اور باز بھی ذبیح کا آلہ ہے
The fruit of the disagreement and the proof of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). A trained dog or trained hawk, when released after reciting tasmiyah, and it goes and wounds the game or it dies, then this game is halal. Like a knife, the dog and hawk are also instruments of slaughter.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling regarding hunting with trained animals. It states that if a trained dog or hawk is sent after reciting tasmiyah (saying 'Bismillah'), and the animal wounds the game causing its death, the game is halal to eat. The analogy is made to a knife: just as a knife is an instrument of slaughter (dhabh), the trained hunting animal is also considered a means of slaughter, provided the hunter intended the hunt and recited tasmiyah. This is the Hanafi position, and the proof is derived from the Quranic verse 'Wa ma allamtum min al-jawarih' (Surah al-Ma'idah: 4). The 'Thamr al-ikhtilaf' refers to a difference of opinion within the school regarding the necessity of the hunter witnessing the hunt; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) have a specific proof which is hinted at here.

Key Terms
کلب معلم (kalb mu'allim): A dog trained for hunting, which responds to commands and does not eat the game.
باز معلم (bāz mu'allim): A trained hawk or falcon used for hunting.
تسمیہ (tasmiyah): Reciting 'Bismillah' (In the name of Allah) upon releasing the hunting animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on game killed by a trained dog that was released with tasmiyah?
A) It is halal because the dog is like a knife.
B) It is halal only if the hunter witnesses the kill.
C) It is haram because the dog is not a valid slaughterer.
D) It is makruh.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a trained hunting animal is like a knife; reciting tasmiyah upon release makes the caught game halal, even if the hunter does not witness the death.

Wounding: A Necessary Condition for Hunting
States that wounding the game is a condition for its permissibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition of wounding for hunting and identify the Quranic proof from 'al-jawarih'.

Core Idea
For game to be halal, the hunting animal must inflict a wound — the Quranic term 'al-jawarih' demands it.

This lesson examines a key condition in Hanafi hunting law: wounding. The Hanafi school uniquely requires that the hunting animal physically wound the game. Our source passage, from the commentary on Al-Hidayah, derives this from the Quranic word 'al-jawarih' in Surah al-Ma'idah 5:4. Let's explore the proof.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Wounding (zakhmī) is a necessary condition (shart) for the hunted game to be halal. If the animal kills without wounding, the game is not halal.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the Quranic proof
The verse 'wa ma allamtum min al-jawarih' (Quran 5:4) is cited as the primary evidence.
The complete verse permits eating what trained hunting animals catch.
3
Explain the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'al-jawarih' is derived from 'jarh' (wounding). The verse linguistically restricts permissible hunting to that which involves wounding.
This is a linguistic argument specific to the Hanafi school.
4
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school does not require wounding as a condition; they interpret 'jawarih' as 'those that earn/hunt' and focus on training.
The Hanafi view relies on the literal meaning of 'jarh'.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi
The Hanafi position requiring wounding is part of the zahir al-riwayah and is the operative ruling for fatwa.
No intra-Hanafi dispute on this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شکار کے لئے زخمی کرنا شرط ہے "وما علمتم من الجوارح" سے زخم پر استدلال
Wounding is a condition for hunting. The proof for wounding is from 'And what you have taught of the hunting animals' (Quran 5:4).
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from the commentary. It asserts a key condition: for game to be halal, the hunting animal must inflict a wound (jarh). This is derived from the Quranic verse 'Wa ma allamtum min al-jawarih' (Surah al-Ma'idah: 4). The Arabic word 'al-jawarih' literally means 'those that earn' or 'those that wound', indicating that the animal must cause a wound. If the animal merely kills by strangling or crushing without wounding, the game is not halal. This is a distinct Hanafi position; other schools may allow killing by a trained animal even without a wound. The Hanafi proof is that the verse specifies 'jawarih' which implies wounding, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said about hunting dogs: 'If you send your dog and mention the name of Allah, then eat what it catches for you, even if the dog eats from it' – but the Hanafis interpret 'eating' as indicating that the dog has a share, but wounding is still needed. This condition is part of the zahir al-riwayah.

Key Terms
زخمی (zakhmī): Wounding; the infliction of a physical injury that causes bleeding.
جوارح (jawārih): Hunting animals; literally 'those that wound'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
From which Quranic word do Hanafi scholars derive the condition of wounding in hunting?
A) 'al-jawarih'
B) 'al-mu'allim'
C) 'al-sayd'
D) 'al-dhabh'

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely conditions hunting on wounding, derived from the Quranic term 'al-jawarih'.

Ruling When a Hunting Dog Eats from Its Prey
Ruling on when a hunting dog eats from the prey and the effect on multiple catches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a hunting dog eats from its prey and distinguish between single and multiple prey cases.

Core Idea
A dog that eats from its prey shows it was hunting for itself, making that prey haram.

When hunting with a dog, the dog's training (ta'līm) is crucial. A trained dog catches prey for its master without consuming it. If the dog eats even a part, it indicates it acted for itself, forfeiting the halal status of that prey. This lesson clarifies the ruling and a key exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger action
The dog or cheetah must actually eat from the prey – it is not enough that it merely bites or mouths it.
Eating means consuming any part – even a small amount.
2
State the default ruling
When the dog eats from the prey, that prey becomes haram (unlawful) because the dog's action shows it was not properly trained.
This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa.
3
Apply the multiple-catch scenario
If the dog caught several animals and then ate from only one of them, that specific prey is haram, but the earlier ones remain halal.
The dog's eating does not retroactively spoil previous catches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کتا یا چیتا شکار سے کھالے تو کھانا جائز نہیں ہے کتے نے چند شکار کئے پھر ایک سے کھایا تو یہ شکار نہیں کھایا جائے گا پہلے پکڑے ہوئے شکاروں کا حکم
If a dog or cheetah eats from the prey, then eating it is not permissible. If a dog caught several preys then ate from one, then that prey will not be eaten. The ruling of previously caught preys.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling: if a hunting dog or cheetah consumes any part of the game it has caught, that game becomes haram (unlawful) to eat. The reasoning is that eating from the prey indicates that the animal is not fully trained (ghayr mu'allam), because a trained hunting animal should not eat the prey; it only catches for its master. If the dog catches multiple animals and then eats from one, only that one becomes haram; the earlier ones remain halal, provided they were not eaten from. This is the madhhab of Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) have a different view: they consider the dog's eating as a sign that it caught the prey for itself, but the ruling differs. The text here states the Hanafi position without mention of the dispute in this excerpt.

Key Terms
کلب غیر معلم (kalb ghayr mu'allim): A dog that is not fully trained; one that eats the prey indicates lack of training.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a trained dog catches three rabbits and then eats from the third one before the hunter arrives?
A) All three rabbits are haram.
B) Only the third rabbit is haram; the first two are halal.
C) All three are halal if tasmiyah was recited.
D) Only the first two are haram.

Key Takeaway
A hunting dog's consumption of part of its catch renders that specific prey haram, but other catches made before the eating remain halal.

Ruling on Hunting Prey with Stable Life: Slaughter Obligation and Conditions
Rule on life status of hunted game and slaughter obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on game with stable life, identifying when slaughter is required and when the kill suffices, and explain the consequence of omitting slaughter when able.

Core Idea
If prey is alive with stable life, the hunter must slaughter it; otherwise, it becomes haram.

This passage addresses a critical distinction in Hanafi fiqh: the life status of game at the moment the hunter reaches it. The ruling hinges on whether the animal possesses 'stable life' (hayāt mustaqirrah) or merely fleeting life. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of hunting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animal's life status
Determine if the prey is alive with stable life (more than minimal life signs) or in death throes. Stable life means the animal can be slaughtered and shows clear signs of life like movement or breathing.
The text distinguishes 'life more than that of a sacrificial animal' which refers to stable life.
2
Check the hunter's ability to slaughter
Assess whether the hunter has a knife and can physically reach the animal's throat to perform dhabh. Ability here means both means and access.
If the animal is dangerous or the hunter lacks a tool, he may be unable.
3
Apply the ruling based on ability
If the hunter is able to slaughter, he must do so; the game is only halal if slaughtered. If unable, the dog's kill is sufficient and the game is halal without additional slaughter.
This is the key rule from the passage.
4
Consider the consequence of omission
If the hunter had the ability to slaughter but deliberately omitted it, the game becomes haram. This is a wajib that must not be neglected.
Omission when able invalidates the permissibility.
5
Recognize the consensus example
The text gives an example where the dog tears the belly but the animal is still alive; if the hunter then slaughters it, it is halal by consensus, confirming that slaughter overrides any apparent injury.
This shows that stable life at time of slaughter suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شکار میں حیات ہو اور ند بوح کی حیات سے زیادہ ہو ذبح پر قدرت ہونے یا نہ ہونے کا حکم شکاری کتے نے شکار کا پیٹ پھاڑ دیا اور جو کچھ اس کے پیٹ میں ہے نکال دیا پھر مالک کے ہاتھ آگیا تو یہ شکار حلال ہے اگر شکار کو ذبح کر دیا اگر چہ وہ مشقوق البط بی تو بالاتفاق یہ شکار حلال ہے
The ruling when the game has life and it is more than the life of a sacrificial animal, and whether one has power to slaughter or not. If the hunter's dog tore the belly of the game and took out what is in its belly, then came into the owner's hand, then this game is halal, if he slaughtered the game, even if it is split-bellied, then by agreement this game is halal.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where the game is still alive after being caught, and the life it possesses is more than the minimal life of an animal that can be slaughtered (i.e., it has significant life signs). The ruling depends on whether the hunter has the ability to slaughter it. If the hunter can slaughter it (i.e., has a knife and can reach the throat), then the game is only halal if it is properly slaughtered. If the hunter does not have the ability to slaughter it (e.g., the animal is still dangerous or the hunter lacks a knife), then the game is halal as killed by the dog. The text gives an example: if the dog has torn the belly and the game comes into the owner's hand, if he then slaughters it (despite the split belly), it is halal by consensus. This is because the animal had sufficient life to be considered alive at the time of slaughter. The condition of 'life more than that of a sacrifice' refers to the fact that an animal that is about to die (like in the death throes) is considered to have minimal life; if it has more than that, it is subject to the requirement of slaughter.

Key Terms
حیات مستقرہ (hayāt mustaqirrah): Stable life; the state of an animal that is clearly alive and can be slaughtered.
ند بوح (nabuh): Appears to refer to 'the life of a sacrificial animal' or the minimal life that allows slaughter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hunter reaches a game that is still alive with stable life and is able to slaughter it, but does not slaughter it, what is the ruling?
A) The game is halal because the dog killed it.
B) The game is haram because slaughter was possible but omitted.
C) The game is halal only if the hunter recites tasmiyah.
D) The game is makruh.

Key Takeaway
If the hunter is able to slaughter a game with stable life, he must do so; otherwise, the game becomes haram.

Conditions for Halal Hunting with Trained Animals
Rulings on multiple hunts by trained animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for halal hunting using trained dogs and falcons, and apply them to distinguish halal from haram kills.

Core Idea
A trained animal's kill is halal only if sent with tasmiyah, wounds the prey, and the hunt is continuous.

Hunting with trained animals is permissible in Islam, but strict conditions apply. The Hanafi school details these rules to ensure the hunt meets the requirements of proper slaughter. This lesson examines a series of rulings from a classical text, clarifying when the kill is halal and when it is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animal and its training
Determine if the animal is mu'allam (trained) – it obeys commands to go and stop. An untrained animal's kill is not halal.
The text mentions 'kalb mu'allam' and 'baz mu'allam'.
2
Check the hunter's action
The hunter must intentionally release the animal with the tasmiyah (Bismillah). If release is unknown or without intention, the kill is not halal.
For falcons, unknown release renders the prey haram.
3
Examine the method of killing
The animal must wound the prey, causing blood flow. If it strangles without wounding, the prey is haram (like carrion).
Strangling without wound is mentioned as a mufsid.
4
Evaluate continuity of the hunt
If the animal kills multiple preys without stopping, all are halal. If it stops after a kill and then kills again, the second is not halal.
The text gives an example: dog kills, then stops, then kills another – second is haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ارشانی کار پایا ریب کرایا یا حلال ہے کسی نے ایک شکار پر کلب معلم چھوڑ اوہ کسی دوسرے جانور کو شکار کر کے لایا تو حلال ہے یا نہیں ایک فعل ذبح پر ایک بسم اللہ کافی ہے اگر فعل متعدد ہو تو بسم اللہ بھی متعدد ہو چیتا شکار پر چھوڑا گھات لگا کر اس نے شکار کو پکڑا اور اس کو قتل کر دیا تو شکار کا کھانا حلال ہے کلب معلم کو شکاری نے چھوڑا اس نے ایک شکار کو مارا پھر دوسرے شکار کو مارا تو دونوں حلال ہیں کتے نے شکار مارا اور اس پر ہزار ہا پھر اس کے بعد دوسرا شکار گزرا اس کو مارا تو دوسرا شکار نہیں کھایا جائے گا معلم بازنے شکار کو پکڑا اور اسکو مارڈالا یہ معلوم نہیں کہ اس کو کسی نے چھوڑا ہے یا نہیں تو شکار نہیں کھایا جائے گا معلم باز نے گھات لگا کر تد بیر کی پھر شکار کو مارڈالا تو یہ شکار حلال ہے کتے نے شکار کا گلا دبا کر مارڈالا اور زخم نہیں کیا تو شکار حرام ہے
[؟] halal. Someone released a trained dog on one prey and it brought another animal, is it halal or not? One Bismillah is sufficient for one slaughter action; if actions are multiple, then Bismillah is also multiple. A cheetah released on prey, lying in wait, caught it and killed it, then eating the prey is halal. A hunter released a trained dog; it killed one prey, then killed another prey, both are halal. A dog killed a prey and then stopped, then after that another prey passed by, it killed it, then the second prey will not be eaten. A trained falcon caught a prey and killed it, it is not known whether anyone released it or not, then the prey will not be eaten. A trained falcon lay in wait and planned then killed the prey, then this prey is halal. A dog strangled the prey and killed it and did not wound it, then the prey is haram.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage contains a series of rulings related to hunting with trained animals. Key points: (1) If a dog catches a different animal than intended, the ruling depends; but the text is unclear. (2) For multiple slaughtering actions, each requires its own Bismillah. (3) A cheetah that lies in wait and kills renders the prey halal. (4) A dog that kills two consecutive preys: both are halal. (5) If the dog kills one, then stops, then kills another, the second is not halal (because the interval breaks the continuity of the hunt). (6) If a falcon kills but it is unknown whether it was released by a hunter, the prey is not halal (the release is a condition). (7) If a falcon plans and kills, it is halal. (8) If a dog strangles without wounding, the prey is haram (since it causes death by choking, not by a wound that allows blood flow). These rulings are based on the conditions of the hunting animal's training and the method of killing.

Key Terms
کلب معلم (kalb mu'allam): A trained dog that obeys commands and hunts as taught.
باز (baz): Falcon or trained bird of prey.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a trained dog kills a prey, then stops, then kills another prey later, what is the ruling on the second prey?
A) Halal
B) Haram
C) Makruh
D) Not mentioned

Key Takeaway
For a trained animal's kill to be halal, the animal must be sent with tasmiyah, wound the prey, and the hunt must be continuous without interruption.

The Ruling on Hunting with a Majusi's Dog
Scenarios on dog owner and caller

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prey caught by a Majusi's dog when a Muslim calls it, and identify the conditions for halal prey.

Core Idea
A Majusi's dog yields halal prey only if a Muslim calls it with tasmiyah.

This lesson examines the status of the dog's owner in hunting. The Hanafi school holds that the decisive factor is who sends the dog and whether tasmiyah is recited. Even a dog belonging to a Majusi (Zoroastrian) or an ignorant person can produce halal prey if called by a Muslim with the requisite tasmiyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dog owner's status
Determine whether the dog belongs to a Muslim, a Majusi, an ignorant person, or someone who deliberately omits tasmiyah.
The owner's faith affects the default ruling but is not absolute.
2
Who calls the dog?
Establish who actually sends the dog after the prey: the owner or another Muslim.
Calling here means releasing the dog while shouting or indicating.
3
Check tasmiyah
Confirm whether tasmiyah (saying 'Bismillah') was recited at the time of release. According to Hanafi, it is wajib; deliberate omission renders the prey unlawful.
If omitted deliberately, the prey is not halal; forgetfulness is excused.
4
Apply the ruling
If a Muslim calls the dog and recites tasmiyah, the prey is halal regardless of the dog's owner. If the owner calls it, the owner must be Muslim and reciting tasmiyah.
This applies only if the dog is trained (mu'allam).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسلمان کے کلب معلم کیسا تھ کلب جاہل یا مجوسی کا کتا یا تارک القسمیہ عامد أوالا کتامل گیا تو کیا حکم ہے رہے دوسرے شریک کتے نے شکار کو زخمی نہیں کیا پہلے کتے نے زخمی کر کے مارڈالا شکار کھایا جائیگا یا نہیں اگر دوسرا کتا پکڑنے میں شریک نہیں تو شکار حلال ہے مسلمان کے کلب معلم کو مجوسی للکار دے تو شکار حلال ہو گا یا نہیں مجوسی کے کتے کو مسلمان للکا ر دے تو شکار کا حکم غیر مرسل کتے کو مسلمان نے للکارا اور تسمیہ پڑھی تو شکار حلال ہے مسلمان نے شکار پر کتا چھوڑا اور شکار کو مار کرست کر دیا پھر دوبارہ اسکو ہلاک کر ڈالا تو یہ شکار حلال ہے دو مسلمان شخصوں کے دو کتے ہیں ایک نے شکار کوست کیا دوسرے نے مارڈالا تو شکار حلال ہے شکار کا مالک کون ہوگا
How is the trained dog of a Muslim? A dog of an ignorant person or a Majusi, or one who deliberately leaves tasmiyah, or not, what is the ruling? If the other partner dog did not wound the prey, and the first dog wounded and killed it, will the prey be eaten or not? If the second dog did not participate in catching, then the prey is halal. If a Majusi calls the Muslim's trained dog, will the prey be halal or not? The ruling on the dog of a Majusi if a Muslim calls it. A Muslim called an untrained dog and recited tasmiyah, then the prey is halal. A Muslim released a dog on prey and the prey was [؟], then again killed it, then this prey is halal. Two Muslims have two dogs; one wounded the prey, the other killed it, then the prey is halal. Who will be the owner of the prey?
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings related to the status of the hunter and the dog. It asks about a dog belonging to a Muslim vs. an ignorant person or a Majusi (fire-worshipper), and about calling the dog. The key principles: If a Muslim calls a non-Muslim's dog or an untrained dog with tasmiyah, the prey is halal. If two dogs are involved, only the one that wounds matters. The final line raises the issue of ownership: the prey belongs to the one who sent the dog or calls it? The text does not provide an explicit answer here, but in Hanafi fiqh, the prey belongs to the one who sent the dog or shot the arrow.

Key Terms
مجوسی (majusi): A Zoroastrian (fire-worshipper).
تارک القسمیہ (tarik al-tasmiyah): One who deliberately omits saying Bismillah.
کلب معلم (kalb mu'allam): A trained hunting dog that catches prey but does not eat it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Hunting with Multiple Dogs and Ownership
Scenarios on multiple dogs and ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine who owns the prey when two dogs are involved, and state the ruling when one dog wounds and the other kills.

Core Idea
Only the dog that wounds the prey matters; the prey belongs to the one who sent the wounding dog.

This lesson addresses scenarios with two or more dogs in hunting. The passage clarifies that if a dog does not participate in catching (i.e., no wound), its presence is irrelevant. The prey is halal if the wounding dog was sent by a Muslim with tasmiyah. Ownership is determined by the sender of the dog that caused the kill.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dogs' actions
Determine which dog wounded the prey and which dog killed it. Only the wounding dog is considered the one that 'caught' it.
If a dog only touches or chases without wounding, it is not a participant in the catch.
2
Check participation in catching
If the second dog did not wound or kill, it is not a partner. The prey is halal from the first dog's catch, provided conditions are met.
The passage says: 'If the second dog did not participate in catching, then the prey is halal.'
3
Determine ownership
The prey belongs to the person who sent the dog that inflicted the fatal wound. If two dogs together wounded, the prey is shared? (Not explicitly stated, but Hanafi fiqh: if both wound, they share; if one wounds and another kills without wound, the wounding dog's owner owns.)
Ownership question is raised at the end: 'Who will be the owner of the prey?' Implicit answer: the one who sent the dog that caused the death, usually the wounder.

Source Text

The General Principle of Shooting: Intention and Capability
General principle from Zahir al-Riwayah on shooting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi principle that a prey caught by shooting is halal only if it was intended and caught by the means and capability.

Core Idea
The prey is halal when the act of hunting is directed at it and caught according to readiness and capability.

In this section, the author establishes the foundational principle for hunting by shooting (ramy). Based on the texts of Zahir al-Riwayah, the prey's permissibility hinges on two key factors: the hunter's intention and the tool's capability. This principle governs all subsequent scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is shooting an arrow or releasing a dog at sensed movement. The ruling is that the prey is halal (permissible) if the act is directed at it and it is caught according to the means.
2
Locate the evidence
The evidence is from Zahir al-Riwayah, the core Hanafi texts. It states: 'When the act of hunting is given (to the tool), then the prey according to readiness and capability will be halal.'
This is a textual proof (dalil naqli) with zanni thubut and zanni dalala.
3
Analyze the conditions
Two conditions emerge: (1) the hunter must direct the act of hunting towards the specific prey (intention), and (2) the tool must catch the prey by its readiness and capability (means). If both are present, the prey is halal.
The condition of capability ensures the tool is effective for hunting that type of prey.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فى الرمي شکاری نے حرکت یا آہٹ محسوس کی پھر تیر مارا یا کتا چھوڑا اور شکار پکڑ لیا حالانکہ وہ تیر سے یا کتے کے پکڑنے سے مرچکا ہے تو کیا حکم ہے ظاہر الروایۃ کی دلیل جب فعل اصطیاد کو دیا جائے تو وہاں مصاب بقد راستعداد وصلاحیت حلال ہوگا کسی پرندہ کی طرف تیر پھینکا وہ بھاگاور دوسرے شکار کو تیر لگ گیا بھاگنے والا اگر شکار ہے تو مصاب حلال ورنہ نہیں مچھلی یا ٹڈی کی طرف تیر پھینکا اور وہ کسی اور شکار کو جالگا تو وہ جانور حلال ہے یا حرام جس کی آہٹ سنی تھی بعینہ تیر اس کو لگا حالانکہ اس کو انسان گمان کرتا تھا جبکہ وہ شکار ہے تو وہ شکار حلال ہے تسمیہ جس طرح ارسال کے لئے ضروری ہے اسی طرح رمی کے لئے بھی ضروری ہے اگر شکار زندہ مل جائے ذبح لازم ہے ورنہ حلال نہ ہوگا
Section on shooting. The hunter sensed movement or sound, then shot an arrow or released a dog, and the prey was caught, while it died from the arrow or the dog's catch, what is the ruling? The evidence from Zahir al-Riwayah: When the act of hunting is given (to the tool), then the prey according to readiness and capability will be halal. He threw an arrow at a bird, it escaped, and the arrow hit another prey. If the escaping one is prey, then the hit one is halal, otherwise not. He threw an arrow at a fish or locust, and it hit another animal, then that animal is halal or haram? The one whose sound he heard, exactly the arrow hit it, even though he thought it was a human, if it is prey, then that prey is halal. Tasmiyah is necessary for shooting just as it is for releasing. If the prey is found alive, slaughter is necessary; otherwise it will not be halal.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a section on shooting (رمي) covering various scenarios. The key ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah: the prey is halal if the act of hunting was directed (intended) towards it, and the prey is caught according to the means and capability. Then it discusses cases where an arrow misses the intended target and hits another: if the original intended prey was a permissible game, then the mistakenly hit animal is halal; if the intended was not game (e.g., fish or locust), then the hit animal is not halal. Also, if the hunter shoots at a sound thinking it might be game, and it turns out to be game, it is halal. Finally, it reiterates that tasmiyah is wajib for shooting, and if the prey is found alive, it must be slaughtered; otherwise it is not halal.

Key Terms
رمی (ramy): Shooting an arrow or projectile at game.
مصاب (musab): The prey that is hit or caught by the hunter's tool.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Zahir al-Riwayah principle, a prey caught by shooting is halal when:
A) The hunter says 'Bismillah' and the arrow hits any animal.
B) The act of hunting is directed at that prey and the tool catches it by its readiness and capability.
C) The hunter intends to hunt generally and the arrow is capable of killing.
D) The prey is a permissible species and the arrow is fired from a bow.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the permissibility of game caught by shooting depends on the hunter's intention and the tool's capability.

Specific Scenarios: Unintended Prey and the Obligation of Tasmiyah
Specific cases: unintended prey, mistaken identity, and the obligation of tasmiyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on unintended prey in shooting and identify the obligation of tasmiyah and slaughter.

Core Idea
An arrow that hits an unintended animal is halal only if the intended target was permissible game; tasmiyah is wajib for shooting, and live prey must be slaughtered.

After establishing the general principle, the text presents several concrete cases. It addresses what happens when the arrow misses the intended target and hits another animal, how to rule when hunting fish or locusts, and the situation when the hunter shoots at a sound. It also emphasizes that tasmiyah is obligatory (wajib) when shooting, just as when releasing a dog, and that any prey found alive must be slaughtered; otherwise, it is not halal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The text mentions three distinct situations: (1) shooting at a bird that escapes and hitting a different prey, (2) shooting at a fish or locust and hitting another animal, and (3) shooting at a sound (thinking it might be human or animal) and hitting game.
Each scenario has a specific ruling based on intention and the nature of the intended target.
2
Apply the rule for unintended prey
When the intended target is a permissible game animal (like a bird), and the arrow hits a different prey, the hit prey is halal. If the intended target is not game (e.g., fish, locust), the hit prey is not halal. This is because the original intention was directed towards a non-game species, so the 'act of hunting' is not considered valid.
Fish and locusts are halal but are not typically hunted with arrows; the text treats them as non-game in this context.
3
Rule for shooting at a sound
If the hunter shoots at a sound, thinking it might be a human, but it turns out to be game, the game is halal. The text states: 'The one whose sound he heard, exactly the arrow hit it, even though he thought it was a human, if it is prey, then that prey is halal.' This indicates that as long as the actual hit animal was the object of the sound (and thus the intended target in a sense), it is halal.
The mistaken belief about its nature does not affect permissibility.
4
Understand the requirement of tasmiyah and slaughter
The text concludes by stating that tasmiyah (saying 'Bismillah') is necessary (wajib) for shooting, just as it is for releasing a dog. Furthermore, if the prey is found alive after being hit, it must be slaughtered (zabihah) in the prescribed manner; otherwise, it is not halal.
This is a standard Hanafi position; omission of tasmiyah renders the prey haram.

Source Text

Section on shooting. The hunter sensed movement or sound, then shot an arrow or released a dog, and the prey was caught, while it died from the arrow or the dog's catch, what is the ruling? The evidence from Zahir al-Riwayah: When the act of hunting is given (to the tool), then the prey according to readiness and capability will be halal. He threw an arrow at a bird, it escaped, and the arrow hit another prey. If the escaping one is prey, then the hit one is halal, otherwise not. He threw an arrow at a fish or locust, and it hit another animal, then that animal is halal or haram? The one whose sound he heard, exactly the arrow hit it, even though he thought it was a human, if it is prey, then that prey is halal. Tasmiyah is necessary for shooting just as it is for releasing. If the prey is found alive, slaughter is necessary; otherwise it will not be halal.

Commentary
This passage is a section on shooting (رمي) covering various scenarios. The key ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah: the prey is halal if the act of hunting was directed (intended) towards it, and the prey is caught according to the means and capability. Then it discusses cases where an arrow misses the intended target and hits another: if the original intended prey was a permissible game, then the mistakenly hit animal is halal; if the intended was not game (e.g., fish or locust), then the hit animal is not halal. Also, if the hunter shoots at a sound thinking it might be game, and it turns out to be game, it is halal. Finally, it reiterates that tasmiyah is wajib for shooting, and if the prey is found alive, it must be slaughtered; otherwise it is not halal.

Key Terms
رمی (ramy): Shooting an arrow or projectile at game.
مصاب (musab): The prey that is hit or caught by the hunter's tool.

Definition of Mawat and the Ruling on Its Revival
Introduces the chapter on reviving dead lands, defining mawat and its ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of mawat and explain that reviving it makes the reviver the owner.

Core Idea
Reviving ownerless, unproductive land grants ownership by the hadith.

This lesson introduces the chapter on reviving dead lands (Ihya al-Mawat). We will examine the definition of mawat according to the Hanafi school and the prophetic basis for the ruling that reviving such land makes the reviver its owner. Understanding this definition is crucial before exploring detailed conditions and disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its linguistic origin
Mawat originally means 'dead animal.' By analogy, land that is ownerless and unproductive is called mawat. Note the analogy: just as a dead animal cannot benefit, this land cannot be benefited from.
Linguistic derivation helps understand the legal concept.
2
Grasp the Shari'ah definition from Quduri
According to Quduri, mawat is land from which benefit cannot be taken due to water being cut off, water overwhelming it, or similar obstacles that prevent cultivation. This is the standard Hanafi definition.
The definition focuses on physical impediments to cultivation.
3
Identify the proof (dalil)
The legislation is based on the hadith: 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is his.' This is a general proof that revival causes ownership.
The hadith is found in Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim.
4
Understand the ruling (hukm)
Reviving mawat is permissible (mubah) and results in ownership for the reviver upon completion of the revival act (building, cultivating, or planting).
Ownership is established without needing permission from the ruler if the land is truly ownerless.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school requires the imam's permission for revival, while the Hanafi school considers it valid without permission (for land outside inhabited areas).
This difference affects practical application in organized states.

Source Text

Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah Volume 14. Chapter of Reviving Dead Lands. Translation: This chapter is about reviving dead lands. Commentary: Most commentators have mentioned that the connection between the previous chapter and this chapter is that in this chapter also there are many rulings of disliked and not disliked, but this reason is defective, as is apparent. Allama Aini mentioned the connection that in this chapter mention of mawat (dead land) which is unbeneficial, and gold, silver, and silk are also unbeneficial. Thus, eating and drinking in gold and silver vessels is forbidden for men and women, and silk's unbeneficial nature is only for men. The difference is that mawat is unbeneficial by custom, while the mentioned items are unbeneficial by Shari'ah. I say... the connection could also be that both aim at abundance of human sustenance, as is apparent. The basis of its legislation is this hadith: 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is his.' And its cause is the connection of appointment to the ruler. And its ruling is that he becomes the owner of the land that he revived. Mawat is originally said of an animal that has died, then it is used for land that has no owner and is unbeneficial. By analogy with the death of an animal, it was named mawat. And revival means, according to custom, utilization and benefit, such as building a structure on the land, or cultivating it, or planting trees. The Shari'ah definition of dead land: He said: Mawat is land from which benefit cannot be taken due to water being cut off from it, or due to water overwhelming it, or similar things that prevent cultivation. It was named that because benefit is invalid from it. Translation: Quduri said: Mawat is land from which benefit cannot be taken due to water being cut off from it, or due to water overwhelming it, or similar things among those that prevent cultivation. It was named with this name because benefit from it is invalid. Commentary: This is the Shari'ah definition of mawat: that it is land which has become unbeneficial for some reason, either due to its salinity, or due to rise of land, or due to change of soil, or due to water being cut off, or due to excess water rising. Then the author mentioned the reason for naming which we have already written at the beginning.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on reviving dead lands (Ihya al-Mawat). It begins by explaining the connection between this chapter and the previous one (on garments and utensils). The commentators mention various reasons for the placement, such as both dealing with rulings of disliked and not disliked, or the common theme of items that are unbeneficial (mawat, gold/silver vessels, silk). The author further suggests that both chapters aim at increasing human sustenance. The legislation for reviving dead land is based on the prophetic hadith: 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is his.' The text then provides a linguistic definition: mawat originally refers to a dead animal, and by analogy, land that is ownerless and unproductive is called mawat. Revival (ihya) means making use of the land by building, cultivating, or planting. The Shari'ah definition from Quduri is given: mawat is land from which no benefit can be taken due to lack of water, flooding, or similar obstacles to cultivation. The commentary emphasizes that the unproductive nature must be due to a physical impediment, not mere custom. This sets the stage for the detailed rulings on reviving dead land according to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
موات (mawāt): Land that is ownerless and unproductive (unbeneficial) due to natural obstacles like lack of water, flooding, salinity, etc.
احياء الموات (iḥyā' al-mawāt): The act of making dead land productive by building, cultivating, or planting, which grants ownership to the reviver.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Mawat is ownerless, unproductive land; reviving it (cultivating, building) makes the reviver the owner, based on the hadith.

Definition and Conditions of Mawat Land
Defining mawat land and its distance condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of mawat land, identify its three key conditions, and apply the shout test to distinguish mawat from non-mawat land.

Core Idea
Mawat land is ownerless, uncultivable, and beyond the range of a shout from the nearest habitation.

This lesson introduces the foundational definition of mawat (dead) land in Hanafi fiqh. Understanding this definition is crucial because reviving mawat land grants ownership, but only if the land meets the Shariah criteria. The following passage from Al-Hidayah and Al-Quduri lays out the precise conditions, including the unique distance test.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'mawat' in the Arabic passage and note that it is originally used for a dead animal, metaphorically applied to land that is ownerless and non-beneficial.
The shift from animal to land is based on shared lack of life/benefit.
2
List the substantive conditions
From al-Quduri's definition, extract the three conditions: (1) the land is not beneficial (no cultivation), (2) it is ownerless (either ancient ruins without known owner, or land possessed in Islam but no specific owner identified), (3) it is far from habitation—specifically, beyond the distance where a shout from the inhabited edge can be heard.
The shout test is a literal measure of distance, not metaphorical.
3
Understand the rationale (illah) for calling it mawat
The name comes from the invalidity of benefit (butlan al-intifa) due to water cut-off, flooding, salinity, etc. This clarifies why such land is considered 'dead' in a legal sense.
The cause is physical or natural, not owner's absence alone.
4
Apply the shout test
Practice the distance condition: Imagine a person standing at the last building of a village and shouting loudly. If the shout is not heard in the land in question, that land qualifies as mawat. If it is heard, the land is within the fana (precincts) and cannot be revived without permission.
The test establishes that the land is not in common use by residents.
5
Recognize what is NOT mawat
Land with a known owner (even if abandoned) and land near the village (fana al-shehr) are excluded. The text explicitly states that revival is invalid if these conditions are missing.
This prevents usurpation of private or communal property.

تشریح یہ موت کی شرعی تعریف ہے کہ وہ زمین موات کہلاتی ہے کہ کسی وجہ سے وہ قابل انتفاع نہ رہی ہو یا تو اس کے نمکین ہونے کی وجہ سے یاریت کے چڑھ جانے کی وجہ سے یا خالص تبدیل ہونے کی وجہ سے یا پانی کے منقطع ہونے یا زیادہ پانی کے چڑھ آنے سے پھر مصنف نے وجہ تسمیہ بتائی جس کو ہم شروع میں تحریر کر چکے ہیں ۔
قال فما كان منها عاديا لا مالك له او كان مملوكا فى الاسلام لا يعرف له مالك بعينه وهو بعيد من القرية بحيث اذا وقف انسان من اقصى العامر فصاح لا يسمع الصوت فيه فهو موات
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا پس جو زمین عادی ہو ( قدیمی ویران ) جس کا مالک نہ ہو یا وہ اسلام میں مقبوض ہو جس کا کوئی معین مالک نہ پہچانا جاتا ہو اور وہ بستی سے اتنی دور ہو کہ جب کوئی انسان آبادی کے آخر میں کھڑا ہو کر چلائے تو اس میں آواز سنائی نہ دے تو وہ موات ہے۔
تشریح موات وہ زمین کہلائے گی کہ قدیم زمانہ سے ویران، پنجر، نا قابل کاشت پڑی ہوئی ہو اور اسکا کوئی مالک نہ ہو یا دار الاسلام میں اس کا مالک تو ہو لیکن کوئی شخص اس کا مالک نہ ہو۔ نیز آبادی سے اتنی دور ہو کہ اگر کوئی شخص آبادی کے آخری حصہ میں کھڑا ہو کر چلائے تو اس زمین تک آواز نہ پہنچے ، اگر یہ شرطیں مفقود ہوں تو اس کو موات نہ کہا جائے گا، اور اس کا احیاء درست نہ ہوگا۔ نیز اگر وہ آبادی کے قریب ہو تو وہ فنا شہر ہے کہ بستی والے جس سے نفع اٹھاتے ہیں اس کا احیاء درست نہ ہوگا۔
Book of Reviving Dead Lands. Translation: This book is about the revival of dead lands. Explanation: Most commentators have mentioned the connection between the book of drinking and the revival of dead lands as follows: that in this too there are many rulings, prohibited and non-prohibited, but this reason is weak, as is apparent. Allama Aini mentioned the connection as: in this book, dead land is mentioned which is not beneficial, and gold, silver, and silk are also not beneficial. Similarly, eating and drinking in gold and silver vessels is prohibited for men and women alike, and silk being non-beneficial is only for men. The only difference is that the non-beneficial nature of dead land is due to custom, while the non-beneficial nature of the mentioned items is due to Shariah. I said: Another possibility for the connection is that the purpose of both is the abundance of human sustenance, as is apparent. The reason for its legislation is this hadith: 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is his.' And its cause is the connection of predetermined sustenance, and its ruling is that the one who revives it becomes the owner of that land. Dead land originally refers to an animal that has died; then it is used for land that has no owner and is not beneficial. By analogy with the death of an animal, the name 'dead' was given. And 'revival' means, according to custom, utilization and benefit, such as building a structure on the land, cultivating it, or planting trees. The Shariah definition of dead land: He (al-Quduri) said: Dead land is that land from which benefit cannot be taken due to the cutting off of water from it, or due to the overwhelming of water upon it, or something similar that prevents cultivation. It is named thus because of the invalidity of benefiting from it. Translation: Al-Quduri stated: Dead land is land from which benefit cannot be taken due to the cutting off of water, or due to the overwhelming of water, or similar things that prevent cultivation. It was named with this name because of the invalidity of benefiting from it. Explanation: This is the Shariah definition: land is called dead when for some reason it is no longer usable—either due to salinity, rising of [؟], change of soil, cutting off of water, or excessive water. Then the author gave the reason for the name, which we have already written. He said: So land that is ancient (old ruins) with no owner, or that was possessed in Islam but no specific owner is known, and it is far from the village such that if a person stands at the edge of the inhabited area and shouts, the sound is not heard in it—that is dead land. Translation: Al-Quduri said: So land that is ancient (old ruins) with no owner, or that was possessed in Islam but no specific owner is known, and it is far from the village such that if a person stands at the extreme end of the inhabited area and shouts, the sound is not heard in it—then it is dead land. Explanation: Dead land is that which has been abandoned, waste, uncultivable from ancient times and has no owner, or in the abode of Islam it has an owner but no person is known to be its owner. Also, it is so far from habitation that if a person stands at the last part of the inhabited area and shouts, the sound does not reach that land. If these conditions are missing, it will not be called dead land, and its revival will not be valid. Also, if it is near the inhabited area, it is the 'fana' of the city, which the residents benefit from; its revival is not valid.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Reviving Dead Lands (Kitab Ihya' al-Mawat). The commentator first discusses the connection between this book and the previous one on drinking (ashriba). Some commentators say both deal with prohibited and non-prohibited rulings, but this is weak. Allama Aini notes that dead land, gold/silver vessels, and silk are all 'non-beneficial' in a sense, though for different reasons. The author adds that both sections aim at the abundance of human sustenance. The primary proof for the legislation of reviving dead land is the hadith: 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is his.' The ruling is that the reviver becomes the owner. The term 'mawat' originally means a dead animal; metaphorically it applies to land that is ownerless and from which no benefit is taken. 'Ihya' (revival) means making it useful through building, cultivation, or planting trees. Al-Quduri's definition follows: dead land is that which cannot be used due to lack of water, flooding, or similar obstacles. The name reflects the loss of benefit. The commentator then clarifies the Shariah definition: land is dead if salinization, flooding, soil change, or water issues prevent use. The text then gives the practical criteria: land that is ancient ruins without an owner, or land that was possessed in Islam but no specific owner is known, and it is so far from the village that if a person at the edge of the inhabited area shouts, the sound is not heard there. This distance condition ensures the land is not in common use. If these conditions are missing, it is not dead land and its revival is invalid. If the land is near habitation, it is considered the 'fana' (precincts) of the town, which residents use; its revival is also invalid because it is not truly dead.

Key Terms
موات (mawāt): Land that is uninhabited, uncultivable, and ownerless, from which no benefit can be taken due to natural or man-made reasons.
احیاء (iḥyāʾ): The act of making dead land useful by constructing, cultivating, or planting trees, thereby establishing ownership.
عادی (ʿādī): Land that has been ruined since ancient times (referring to the people of 'Ad), i.e., so old that no owner is known.
فناء شہر (fanāʾ al-shehr): The immediate precincts of a town, which are used by residents for purposes like dumping rubbish or grazing animals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this lesson, which of the following is a necessary condition for land to be considered mawat?
A) It must be owned by a known Muslim.
B) It must be so far from the village that a shout from the edge of the inhabited area is not heard there.
C) It must be near a water source.
D) It must have been cultivated within the last year.

Key Takeaway
Mawat land is defined by ownerlessness, non-benefit, and a minimum distance from habitation measured by the shout test; reviving it grants ownership only if all conditions are met.

The Ownership Condition for Mawat Land: Imam Muhammad's Addition
Expanding the definition of mawat land by adding the condition of not being owned by a Muslim or dhimmi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Muhammad's condition for mawat land and apply it to distinguish between ownable dead land and owned waste land.

Core Idea
Mawat land must be ownerless; if owned by a Muslim or dhimmi, it retains ownership and cannot be revived without permission.

In this passage from al-Hidayah, the author presents an important refinement to the definition of mawat (dead) land. While al-Quduri described mawat as land that is ancient ruins with unknown owner, Imam Muhammad adds a key condition: the land must not be owned by a Muslim or a dhimmi. This lesson examines the implications of this condition and the rules that follow when the owner later appears.

Guided Path
1
Understand the baseline definition
Identify al-Quduri's definition of mawat land as mentioned in the text: land that has been ruined for a long time (adi) with unknown owner.
2
Identify Imam Muhammad's added condition
Note the condition from Imam Muhammad's riwaya: the land must also not be owned by a Muslim or a dhimmi, even if benefit from the land has ceased.
3
Apply the condition
Distinguish between land that is owned (even if waste) and land that is truly mawat. Owned land is not mawat and cannot be revived without permission.
4
Trace the consequences
If the owner is unknown, the land belongs to the community of Muslims. If the owner appears after cultivation, the land is returned to the owner and the cultivator must compensate for any decrease in value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عادی کا معنیٰ اور ارض موات کی شرط
قال رضى الله عنه هكذا ذكره القدورى ومعنى العادى ماقدم خرابه والمروى عن محمد انه يشترط ان لا يكون مملوكا المسلم او ذمى مع انقطاع الارتفاق بها لتكون ميتة مطلقا فاما التي هي مملوكة لمسلم اوذمى لا تكون موانا واذا لم يعرف مالكه يكون لجماعة المسلمين ولو ظهر له مالک ترد عليه ويضمن الزارع نقصانها
ترجمہ حضرت مصنف صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ ایسے ہی اسکو قدوری نے ذکر کیا ہے اور عادی کے معنی یہ ہیں جس کا خراب ہونا قدیمی ہو اور امام محمد سے مروی ہے کہ شرط یہ ہے کہ مطلقا مردار ہو۔ پس بہر حال وہ زمین جو کسی مسلمان یا زمی کی مملوک ہو وہ مولت نہیں ہوگی اور جب اس کا مالک معلوم نہ ہو تو وہ جماعت مسلمین کے لئے ہوگی اور اگر اس کا مالک ظاہر ہو جائے تو اس پر واپس کر دی جائے گی اور کاشتکار اس کے نقصان کا ضامن ہوگا۔ تشریح امام قدوری نے وہ بیان فرمایا جو متن میں مذکور ہوا لیکن امام محمد نے یہ شرط لگائی ہے کہ زمین کے ناقابل انتفاع ہونے کے ساتھ ساتھ یہ بھی شرط ہے کہ وہ کسی مسلمان یا ذمی کی مملوک نہ ہو ورنہ وہ موات نہ ہو گی اور اگر اس کا کوئی مالک معلوم نہ ہوتو وہ جماعت مسلمین کیلئے ہے کسی ایک کو اس کے مالک بننے کا حق نہ ہوگا۔ اگر کسی نے زمین میں کاشت کی پھر اس کا مالک ظاہر ہو گیا تو وہ زمین مالک کو واپس کر دی جائے گی اور کسان اس کے نقصان کا ضمان مالک کو دے گا۔
تنبیہ - عادی۔ عاد کی جانب منسوب ہے جس سے پرانی ہونے کا کنایہ کیا گیا ہے کیونکہ عاد پہلے زمانہ میں تھا تو عادی اس کو کہا گیا ہے کہ جو قدیمی ویران ہو اور اس کا کوئی مالک معلوم نہ ہو۔
The meaning of 'adi and the condition of dead land. He (the author of Hidayah) said: This is how al-Quduri mentioned it, and the meaning of 'adi is that which has been ruined for a long time. And it is reported from Muhammad (Imam Muhammad) that the condition is that it is absolutely dead. So, the land that is owned by a Muslim or a dhimmi is not dead land. And if its owner is not known, it belongs to the community of Muslims. And if its owner appears, it will be returned to him, and the cultivator will guarantee the decrease (in its value). Translation: The author of al-Hidayah said: This is how al-Quduri mentioned it, and the meaning of 'adi is that which has been ruined for a long time. And it is reported from Muhammad that he stipulates that it should not be owned by a Muslim or a dhimmi, along with the cessation of benefit from it, so that it is absolutely dead. As for land that is owned by a Muslim or a dhimmi, it is not dead land. And if its owner is not known, it belongs to the community of Muslims. And if its owner appears, it will be returned to him, and the cultivator will guarantee the decrease (in its value). Explanation: Imam al-Quduri stated what is mentioned in the text. But Imam Muhammad added this condition: along with the land being non-beneficial, it must also not be owned by a Muslim or a dhimmi; otherwise, it will not be dead land. And if its owner is not known, it belongs to the community of Muslims; no individual has the right to become its owner. If someone cultivated the land and then its owner appears, the land will be returned to the owner, and the farmer will give compensation for the damage to the owner. Note: 'Adi is attributed to 'Ad, by which antiquity is implied, because 'Ad was in ancient times. So 'adi is called that which is old ruins and its owner is not known.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the conditions for land to be considered mawat. The author of al-Hidayah first explains the meaning of 'adi (ancient ruins) as land that has been desolate for a long time. He then quotes a riwaya from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, who adds an important condition: the land must not be owned by a Muslim or a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state). This goes beyond the earlier definition from al-Quduri, which only mentioned the land being ownerless or having an unknown owner. According to Imam Muhammad, if the land is owned by a Muslim or dhimmi, even if it is currently waste and unusable, it is not mawat. This is because ownership persists, and the land cannot be revived without the owner's permission. If the owner is unknown, the land belongs to the collective community of Muslims (bayt al-mal). An individual cannot simply take it and claim ownership. If someone does cultivate it and later the owner appears, the land is returned to the owner, and the cultivator (zari') must compensate the owner for any decrease in the land's value (nusqan). This ensures that ownership rights are protected. The passage concludes with a linguistic note: 'adi is derived from 'Ad, an ancient people, indicating that the land has been ruined since time immemorial and no owner is known.

Key Terms
ذمی (dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject living under the protection of the Islamic state.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage; in this context, the cultivator must pay for the decrease in land value to the owner if the land is later claimed.
نقصان (nuqṣān): The decrease in the land's value or usability caused by the cultivator's actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'adi mean?
A) Land that is owned.
B) Land that has been ruined for a long time.
C) Land that is irrigated.
D) Land that is barren.

Key Takeaway
Mawat land must be ownerless in the sense that it is not owned by a Muslim or dhimmi; ownership persists even if the land is waste, and reviving owned land without permission is a violation.
